<meta name="DC.Description" content="Convergent and parallel evolution (homoplasy) is widespread in the tree of life and can obscure evidence about phylogenetic relationships. Homoplasy can be elevated in genomes because individual loci may have independent evolutionary histories different from the species history. We sequenced the genomes of five cacti, including the iconic saguaro of the Sonoran Desert and three other columnar cacti, to investigate whether previously uncharacterized features of genome evolution might explain long-standing challenges to understanding cactus phylogeny. We found that 60% of the amino acid sites in proteins exhibiting homoplasy do so because of conflicts between gene genealogies and species histories. This phenomenon, termed hemiplasy, is likely a consequence of the unusually long generation time of these cacti." />

<meta name="article:published_time" content="2017-11-07" />
<meta name="article:section" content="Biological Sciences" />
<meta name="citation_title" content="Extensive gene tree discordance and hemiplasy shaped the genomes of North American columnar cacti" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Few clades of plants have proven as difficult to classify as cacti. One explanation may be an unusually high level of convergent and parallel evolution (homoplasy). To evaluate support for this phylogenetic hypothesis at the molecular level, we sequenced the genomes of four cacti in the especially problematic tribe Pachycereeae, which contains most of the large columnar cacti of Mexico and adjacent areas, including the iconic saguaro cactus (&lt;i&gt;Carnegiea gigantea&lt;/i&gt;) of the Sonoran Desert. We assembled a high-coverage draft genome for saguaro and lower coverage genomes for three other genera of tribe Pachycereeae (&lt;i&gt;Pachycereus&lt;/i&gt;, &lt;i&gt;Lophocereus&lt;/i&gt;, and &lt;i&gt;Stenocereus&lt;/i&gt;) and a more distant outgroup cactus, &lt;i&gt;Pereskia&lt;/i&gt;. We used these to construct 4,436 orthologous gene alignments. Species tree inference consistently returned the same phylogeny, but gene tree discordance was high: 37% of gene trees having at least 90% bootstrap support conflicted with the species tree. Evidently, discordance is a product of long generation times and moderately large effective population sizes, leading to extensive incomplete lineage sorting (ILS). In the best supported gene trees, 58% of apparent homoplasy at amino sites in the species tree is due to gene tree-species tree discordance rather than parallel substitutions in the gene trees themselves, a phenomenon termed “hemiplasy.” The high rate of genomic hemiplasy may contribute to apparent parallelisms in phenotypic traits, which could confound understanding of species relationships and character evolution in cacti.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Convergent and parallel evolution (homoplasy) is widespread in the tree of life and can obscure evidence about phylogenetic relationships. Homoplasy can be elevated in genomes because individual loci may have independent evolutionary histories different from the species history. We sequenced the genomes of five cacti, including the iconic saguaro of the Sonoran Desert and three other columnar cacti, to investigate whether previously uncharacterized features of genome evolution might explain long-standing challenges to understanding cactus phylogeny. We found that 60% of the amino acid sites in proteins exhibiting homoplasy do so because of conflicts between gene genealogies and species histories. This phenomenon, termed hemiplasy, is likely a consequence of the unusually long generation time of these cacti.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Extensive gene tree discordance and hemiplasy shaped the genomes of North American columnar cacti" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/content/114/45/12003/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Convergent and parallel evolution (homoplasy) is widespread in the tree of life and can obscure evidence about phylogenetic relationships. Homoplasy can be elevated in genomes because individual loci may have independent evolutionary histories different from the species history. We sequenced the genomes of five cacti, including the iconic saguaro of the Sonoran Desert and three other columnar cacti, to investigate whether previously uncharacterized features of genome evolution might explain long-standing challenges to understanding cactus phylogeny. We found that 60% of the amino acid sites in proteins exhibiting homoplasy do so because of conflicts between gene genealogies and species histories. This phenomenon, termed hemiplasy, is likely a consequence of the unusually long generation time of these cacti." />
<meta name="og-title" property="og:title" content="Extensive gene tree discordance and hemiplasy shaped the genomes of North American columnar cacti" />
<meta name="og-url" property="og:url" content="https://www.pnas.org/content/114/45/12003" />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="Convergent and parallel evolution (homoplasy) is widespread in the tree of life and can obscure evidence about phylogenetic relationships. Homoplasy can be elevated in genomes because individual loci may have independent evolutionary histories different from the species history. We sequenced the genomes of five cacti, including the iconic saguaro of the Sonoran Desert and three other columnar cacti, to investigate whether previously uncharacterized features of genome evolution might explain long-standing challenges to understanding cactus phylogeny. We found that 60% of the amino acid sites in proteins exhibiting homoplasy do so because of conflicts between gene genealogies and species histories. This phenomenon, termed hemiplasy, is likely a consequence of the unusually long generation time of these cacti." />

</ul></div><div class="author-tooltip-6"><div class="author-tooltip-name">Seunghee Lee </div><div class="author-tooltip-affiliation"><span class="author-tooltip-text"><div class='author-affiliation'><span class='nlm-sup'>a</span>Arizona Genomics Institute, School of Plant Sciences, <span class='nlm-institution'>University of Arizona</span>, Tucson, <span class='nlm-addr-line'>AZ</span> 85721;</div></span></div><ul class="author-tooltip-find-more"><li class="author-tooltip-gs-link first"><a href="/lookup/google-scholar?link_type=googlescholar&amp;gs_type=author&amp;author%5B0%5D=Seunghee%2BLee%2B" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Find this author on Google Scholar</a></li>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-4">Convergent and parallel evolution (homoplasy) is widespread in the tree of life and can obscure evidence about phylogenetic relationships. Homoplasy can be elevated in genomes because individual loci may have independent evolutionary histories different from the species history. We sequenced the genomes of five cacti, including the iconic saguaro of the Sonoran Desert and three other columnar cacti, to investigate whether previously uncharacterized features of genome evolution might explain long-standing challenges to understanding cactus phylogeny. We found that 60% of the amino acid sites in proteins exhibiting homoplasy do so because of conflicts between gene genealogies and species histories. This phenomenon, termed hemiplasy, is likely a consequence of the unusually long generation time of these cacti.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-5">Few clades of plants have proven as difficult to classify as cacti. One explanation may be an unusually high level of convergent and parallel evolution (homoplasy). To evaluate support for this phylogenetic hypothesis at the molecular level, we sequenced the genomes of four cacti in the especially problematic tribe Pachycereeae, which contains most of the large columnar cacti of Mexico and adjacent areas, including the iconic saguaro cactus (<em>Carnegiea gigantea</em>) of the Sonoran Desert. We assembled a high-coverage draft genome for saguaro and lower coverage genomes for three other genera of tribe Pachycereeae (<em>Pachycereus</em>, <em>Lophocereus</em>, and <em>Stenocereus</em>) and a more distant outgroup cactus, <em>Pereskia</em>. We used these to construct 4,436 orthologous gene alignments. Species tree inference consistently returned the same phylogeny, but gene tree discordance was high: 37% of gene trees having at least 90% bootstrap support conflicted with the species tree. Evidently, discordance is a product of long generation times and moderately large effective population sizes, leading to extensive incomplete lineage sorting (ILS). In the best supported gene trees, 58% of apparent homoplasy at amino sites in the species tree is due to gene tree-species tree discordance rather than parallel substitutions in the gene trees themselves, a phenomenon termed “hemiplasy.” The high rate of genomic hemiplasy may contribute to apparent parallelisms in phenotypic traits, which could confound understanding of species relationships and character evolution in cacti.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/saguaro" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-saguaro" rel="nofollow">Saguaro</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/homoplasy" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-homoplasy" rel="nofollow">homoplasy</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/lineage-sorting" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-lineage-sorting" rel="nofollow">lineage sorting</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/phylogenomics" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-phylogenomics" rel="nofollow">phylogenomics</a></li></ul><p id="p-6" class="flushleft">Cactaceae have undergone adaptive radiation on a continental scale in the Americas. Occurring from arid deserts to alpine steppes and tropical forests, they exhibit a remarkable diversity of growth forms, ranging from tiny, nearly subterranean “buttons” to giant columnar or candelabra forms, epiphytes, and leafy shrubs (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). Classification of the family’s 1,438 species (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>) has been unusually fraught, with taxonomic treatments recognizing anywhere from 20 to 233 genera (<a id="xref-ref-1-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a><a id="xref-ref-2-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-2"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-3"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>), and classification above the genus level being equally problematic (<a id="xref-ref-1-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>, <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>). In part this has been attributed to homoplasy (ref. <a id="xref-ref-1-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>, p. 18 and ref. <a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>, p. 45), the independent evolutionary origin of the same trait (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). For example, early taxonomists combined the giant columnar cacti of North and South America into one large genus <em>Cereus</em> Mill (<a id="xref-ref-3-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>). Later split into numerous smaller genera, this association was sometimes maintained at a suprageneric rank (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>), but molecular phylogenies clearly show North and South American <em>Cereus</em> are separate clades (<a id="xref-ref-6-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>, <a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>).</p><p id="p-7">Frequent convergence in growth habit may reflect design limitation (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>) of the relatively simple cactus body plan of stem succulence, simplified branching, and loss or reduction of leaves (<a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>) (ref. <a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>, p. 536). In cacti, convergent simplification via paedomorphosis (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>), along with parallelisms among close relatives, has also obscured phylogeny (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>, <a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>). This may help explain the finding that &lt;50% of (nonmonotypic) cactus genera are monophyletic (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>). Taxonomic impediments take on special significance in cacti because of the unusually high fraction of species of conservation concern (31% estimated in ref. <a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>). Despite its potential significance, homoplasy has been quantified in cacti for only a small number of phenotypic traits (<a id="xref-ref-14-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>, <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>). Here, we focus on the genomes of cacti and assess the degree to which apparent homoplasy among these species is elevated due to discordance between gene trees and the species tree.</p><p id="p-8">An important but recently recognized contributor to molecular homoplasy is “hemiplasy” (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>, <a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>), originally defined as the apparent multiple origin of a character state on the inferred species tree arising when an inferred gene tree (with no homoplasy) is discordant with that species tree (<a id="xref-ref-18-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a><a id="xref-ref-19-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-19"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-20"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>). A slight generalization of this definition is needed to account for characters with homoplasy on both trees (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>, <em>Lower Right</em>): A character (site) exhibits hemiplasy if the inferred number of character state changes on the species tree is strictly greater than on the gene tree, which occurs only if the two trees are discordant (<a id="xref-sec-7-1" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-7"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a>).</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Hemiplasy in amino acid alignments of cacti (genes annotated as Cgig1_gene##:site ##). Hemiplasy occurs when the inferred number of state changes of a trait is greater on the species tree than on the gene tree, which happens only if the two trees are discordant. Gene trees in blue are imbedded in black species trees. Yellow dot is the position of a state change inferred for an amino acid site for this gene tree. Black rectangles are locations where state changes would be inferred on the species tree (alternative equally optimal reconstructions are possible but the number of state changes is the same). Amino acids are indicated at leaf nodes. Three trees have the same number of state changes on species and gene trees, but at Lower Left a homology (one change) on the gene tree is seen as homoplasy (two changes) on the species tree because of discordance (i.e., hemiplasy). Cgig, C. gigantea; Lsch, L. schottii; Phum, P humboldtii; Ppri, P. pringlei; Sthu, S. thurberi." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-208059519" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Hemiplasy in amino acid alignments of cacti (genes annotated as Cgig1_gene##:site ##). Hemiplasy occurs when the inferred number of state changes of a trait is greater on the species tree than on the gene tree, which happens only if the two trees are discordant. Gene trees in blue are imbedded in black species trees. Yellow dot is the position of a state change inferred for an amino acid site for this gene tree. Black rectangles are locations where state changes would be inferred on the species tree (alternative equally optimal reconstructions are possible but the number of state changes is the same). Amino acids are indicated at leaf nodes. Three trees have the same number of state changes on species and gene trees, but at &lt;em&gt;Lower Left&lt;/em&gt; a homology (one change) on the gene tree is seen as homoplasy (two changes) on the species tree because of discordance (i.e., hemiplasy). Cgig, &lt;em&gt;C. gigantea&lt;/em&gt;; Lsch, &lt;em&gt;L. schottii&lt;/em&gt;; Phum, &lt;em&gt;P humboldtii&lt;/em&gt;; Ppri, &lt;em&gt;P. pringlei&lt;/em&gt;; Sthu, &lt;em&gt;S. thurberi&lt;/em&gt;.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="345"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="345"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>

<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/540705" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-9" class="first-child">Hemiplasy in amino acid alignments of cacti (genes annotated as Cgig1_gene##:site ##). Hemiplasy occurs when the inferred number of state changes of a trait is greater on the species tree than on the gene tree, which happens only if the two trees are discordant. Gene trees in blue are imbedded in black species trees. Yellow dot is the position of a state change inferred for an amino acid site for this gene tree. Black rectangles are locations where state changes would be inferred on the species tree (alternative equally optimal reconstructions are possible but the number of state changes is the same). Amino acids are indicated at leaf nodes. Three trees have the same number of state changes on species and gene trees, but at <em>Lower Left</em> a homology (one change) on the gene tree is seen as homoplasy (two changes) on the species tree because of discordance (i.e., hemiplasy). Cgig, <em>C. gigantea</em>; Lsch, <em>L. schottii</em>; Phum, <em>P humboldtii</em>; Ppri, <em>P. pringlei</em>; Sthu, <em>S. thurberi</em>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-10">Genome scale datasets revealed high levels of gene tree discordance (<a id="xref-ref-20-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>, <a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a><a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-23"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>), which suggests a potentially important role for hemiplasy as a contributor to overall molecular homoplasy. Discordance can arise because of incomplete lineage sorting (ILS) and gene flow, which depend, in turn, on demographic factors at the population level, phylogenetic history, divergence times, mutation rate, generation time, and the timing and taxa involved in introgression or hybridization (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>). It can also arise from gene duplication and loss (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>).</p><p id="p-11">The cactus tribe Pachycereeae is a model for taxonomic and phylogenetic complexities in cacti (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>, <a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). Its ∼70 species, including most of the columnar cacti of Mexico and adjacent regions, have been dispersed among 6–23 genera in various taxonomic treatments (<a id="xref-ref-1-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>, <a id="xref-ref-5-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>, <a id="xref-ref-27-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>). Despite early molecular support for its monophyly (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>), broadly sampled recent molecular studies have led to abandonment of the tribe (<a id="xref-ref-9-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>, <a id="xref-ref-13-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>, <a id="xref-ref-15-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>), or to recasting it with the informal name, “core Pachycereeae” (<a id="xref-ref-6-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>). Notably, homoplasy in vegetative and floral traits has been cited as a factor contributing to its difficult taxonomic history (ref. <a id="xref-ref-28-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>, p. 1086 and ref. <a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>, p. 556).</p><p id="p-12">To sample widely in Pachycereeae, we sequenced the genomes of four representatives of the two subtribes of Pachycereeae recognized in Gibson and Horak (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>) (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table S1</a>) and some earlier treatments. This included the iconic saguaro cactus of the Sonoran Desert (<em>Carnegiea gigantea</em>) to serve as a high-coverage reference assembly, and <em>Pachycereus pringlei</em> (cardón, sahueso), <em>Lophocereus schottii</em> (senita), and <em>Stenocereus thurberi</em> (organ pipe, pitaya), also of the Sonoran Desert, to lower coverage. <em>Pereskia humboldtii</em>, a leafy, Andean cactus, was included as an outgroup (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float type-supplementary-material"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/540185/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/540185/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table S1.</span> <p id="p-13" class="first-child">Sample, voucher, and sequence library information for taxa in this paper</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div class="section results-discussion" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results and Discussion</h2><p id="p-16" class="flushleft">The assembly of saguaro’s 1.4-Gb genome (<a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>) from short read libraries (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table S1</a>) spanned 980 Mb, with a scaffold N50 of 61.5 kb (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table S2</a>). Transcriptomes were also assembled (<a id="xref-table-wrap-3-1" class="xref-table" href="#T3">Table S3</a>) and used with other evidence to annotate the saguaro genome. The saguaro genome contains 28,292 protein coding genes; 58% of the assembly consists of transposable elements and retroviral and repeated sequences (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-2" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Tables S2</a> and <a id="xref-table-wrap-4-1" class="xref-table" href="#T4">S4</a>). Assemblies from single libraries of the other four cacti were more fragmented (<a id="xref-table-wrap-5-1" class="xref-table" href="#T5">Table S5</a>).</p><div id="T2" class="table pos-float type-supplementary-material"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/540455/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/540455/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table S2.</span> <p id="p-17" class="first-child"><em>C. gigantea</em> (SGP5) assembly metrics (percent of assembled genome included where appropriate)</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="T3" class="table pos-float type-supplementary-material"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/540249/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/540249/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table S3.</span> <p id="p-18" class="first-child">Saguaro transcriptome sequence and assembly results</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="T4" class="table pos-float type-supplementary-material"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/540394/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/540394/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table S4.</span> <p id="p-19" class="first-child">Repeat annotation of the saguaro genome</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><div id="T5" class="table pos-float type-supplementary-material"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/540419/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/540419/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table S5.</span> <p id="p-20" class="first-child">Comparison of assembly results for five cacti in this study</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-21">The high completeness of the gene space in the saguaro assembly (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-3" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table S2</a>) let us construct 4,436 alignments of orthologous genes across the five cacti, each of which contained two or more exons for all five taxa, and contiguous introns. Based on gene tree confidence levels estimated from alignments containing all nucleotide positions, sets of alignments differing in phylogenetic robustness (“gene confidence sets”) were compiled for downstream analyses. For example, the 90% gene confidence set comprised 458 genes, with gene trees having maximum likelihood bootstrap support value above 90% for all clades (<a id="xref-table-wrap-6-1" class="xref-table" href="#T6">Table 1</a>). These gene sets provide a control for the effect of weakly supported gene trees on gene tree discordance estimates (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>).</p><div id="T6" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/540298/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/540298/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <p id="p-22" class="first-child">Gene tree discordance relative to the Pachycereeae species tree (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a>)</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-27">The most robust 90% gene confidence set implies levels of gene tree discordance between Pachycereeae genera (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>A</em></a>) comparable to those seen in closely related short-lived angiosperm genera such as <em>Solanum</em> sect. <em>Lycopersicon</em> [time scale of 2 million years (MYR); ref. <a id="xref-ref-24-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>] and the <em>Oryza</em> AA genome clade (2.5 MYR: ref. <a id="xref-ref-22-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>). Species trees constructed using both gene tree-based (MP-EST; ref. <a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>) and alignment-based methods (BPP; ref. <a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>) were the same for all gene tree confidence sets and for three different partitioning schemes for the alignments by codon position and intron (<a id="xref-sec-7-2" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-7"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a> and <a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a>). An exhaustive search of tree space indicated a single optimal species tree under the MP-EST pseudolikelihood criterion, and three replicate runs of each MCMC chain in BPP from random starting trees generated this same species tree for all five gene confidence sets, indicating convergence. This optimal tree agreed with previous molecular phylogenetic analyses of mostly plastid genes (<a id="xref-ref-6-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>, <a id="xref-ref-28-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>, <a id="xref-ref-29-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). In the 80% and 90% gene confidence sets, 61% and 63% of gene trees, respectively, agreed with the species tree (<a id="xref-table-wrap-6-2" class="xref-table" href="#T6">Table 1</a>). Estimates of species tree edge lengths for these gene confidence sets in BPP ranged from 1.11 to 1.24 for edge A and from 1.22 to 1.34 for edge B, in “coalescent time units” (= <em>T</em>/2<em>N</em><sub>e</sub>, where <em>T</em> is measured in generations), depending on data partition, with slightly lower values estimated by MP-EST (<a id="xref-table-wrap-6-3" class="xref-table" href="#T6">Table 1</a>).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="(A) Topological discordance in the 90% gene confidence set of 458 gene trees visualized in DensiTree (78). Blue trees are the most frequent, followed by red and shades of green. (B) Species tree inferred with BPP and MP-EST, which was the same for all gene sets, with divergence times estimated using the Neutral partition. Blue dashed line indicates reticulation, having inferred inheritance probability, &#x3B3;, on the optimal PhyloNet analysis of the same set of gene trees. Green dashed line indicates position of reticulation on the next best inferred network (Fig. S1). The origin of this reticulation edge earlier than its endpoint implies the existence of an extinct or unsampled taxon." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-208059519" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;(&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Topological discordance in the 90% gene confidence set of 458 gene trees visualized in DensiTree (78). Blue trees are the most frequent, followed by red and shades of green. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Species tree inferred with BPP and MP-EST, which was the same for all gene sets, with divergence times estimated using the Neutral partition. Green dashed line indicates position of reticulation on the next best inferred network (Fig. S1). The origin of this reticulation edge earlier than its endpoint implies the existence of an extinct or unsampled taxon.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F2.medium.gif" width="310" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 2." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F2.medium.gif" width="310" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/540437" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-28" class="first-child">(<em>A</em>) Topological discordance in the 90% gene confidence set of 458 gene trees visualized in DensiTree (<a id="xref-ref-78-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">78</a>). Blue trees are the most frequent, followed by red and shades of green. (<em>B</em>) Species tree inferred with BPP and MP-EST, which was the same for all gene sets, with divergence times estimated using the Neutral partition. Blue dashed line indicates reticulation, having inferred inheritance probability, γ, on the optimal PhyloNet analysis of the same set of gene trees. Green dashed line indicates position of reticulation on the next best inferred network (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. S1</a>). The origin of this reticulation edge earlier than its endpoint implies the existence of an extinct or unsampled taxon.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-29">Introgression may also contribute to gene tree discordance. Network reconstruction allowing both ILS and gene flow in PhyloNet (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>) indicated substantial support for a network model with one reticulation vs. a tree model (ΔAIC = 17.0 and 28.6 for the 90% and 80% gene sets, respectively; ref. <a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>), implicating introgression into <em>P. pringlei</em> from either <em>S. thurberi</em> or some more closely related but unsampled or extinct taxon (<a id="xref-fig-2-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a> and <a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. S1</a>). The best two networks were the same in both gene confidence sets (although the ranking reversed; <a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. S1</a>). Moreover, the “major tree” within these networks (<a id="xref-sec-7-3" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-7"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a>) was the same as that inferred by species tree methods, and the inheritance probability estimate from the 90% gene confidence set, which indicates the level of introgression, was low (6.5% and 14.2% for the optimal and next best networks in the 90% gene confidence set; although higher in one of the two networks from the 80% gene set). The two optimal networks were consistently recovered in multiple searches from different starting networks, and in each gene set, the two results were substantially better than other suboptimal networks (<a id="xref-fig-3-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. S1</a>).</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-supplementary-material  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="PhyloNet (36) network reconstructions for the 90% (A) and 80% (B) gene confidence sets. Likelihood scores decrease from left to right. AIC scores are relative to the optimal networks at Left. Branch lengths are not scaled to sequence divergence, but the lesser of the two inheritance probabilities for reticulation edges is given next to edge in the format ##.#%. Parametric bootstrap probabilities are given next to edges for top scoring networks in A and B, as percentages in the format ##. The dotted box encloses the two best networks in each gene confidence set, and these are the same in A and B, but in reverse ranking. The remaining networks have substantially worse AIC scores relative to the optimal networks in each row." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-208059519" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;PhyloNet (36) network reconstructions for the 90% (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) and 80% (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) gene confidence sets. Likelihood scores decrease from left to right. AIC scores are relative to the optimal networks at &lt;em&gt;Left&lt;/em&gt;. Branch lengths are not scaled to sequence divergence, but the lesser of the two inheritance probabilities for reticulation edges is given next to edge in the format ##.#%. The dotted box encloses the two best networks in each gene confidence set, and these are the same in &lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;, but in reverse ranking. The remaining networks have substantially worse AIC scores relative to the optimal networks in each row.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. S1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="300"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. S1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="300"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/45/12003/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. S1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
